   - Since CD is horizontal, its perpendicular bisector is vertical line x = (x_C + x_D)/2 = ( -r1 + d + r2 )/2 = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

Intersection with x = x_P yields s = x_P / y_P (provided y_P ≠ 0). Then y-coordinate y_H = s (d - x_P) = x_P (d - x_P) / y_P.
   - Thus H = (x_P, x_P (d - x_P) / y_P). (We'll later maybe simplify using expressions.)

   So |P|^2 = x_P^2 (1 + (x0 + a)^2 / y0^2) = x_P^2 * (y0^2 + (x0 + a)^2) / y0^2.

   As we computed, y0^2 + (x0 + a)^2 = 2 a (a + x0). So

   So |v|^2 = 2 a x_P^2 (a + x0) / y0^2 + a^2 + 2 a x_P.

   Let’s compute:

   v_x = x_P - x0.
   v_y = y_P - y0.

   We know y_P = - (x_P (x0 + a))/y0.

   |P|^2 = x_P^2 * \frac{2 a (a + x0)}{y0^2}.

   Then |v|^2 = x_P^2 * \frac{2 a (a + x0)}{y0^2} + a^2 + 2 a x_P.

   y0^2 = a^2 - \frac{(4 + a^2 - b^2)^2}{16}.

   y0^2 = \frac{16 a^2 - (4 + a^2 - b^2)^2}{16}.

   Compute the numerator:

   16 a^2 - (a^2 + 4 - b^2)^2 = 16 a^2 - ( (a^2 + 4)^2 - 2 (a^2+4) b^2 + b^4 ) = 16 a^2 - (a^4 + 8 a^2 + 16) + 2 (a^2+4) b^2 - b^4.

   Let's think about the orthocentre H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on the x-axis, H's y-coordinate we expressed as (x_P (2 - x_P)) / y_P. That seems to involve y_P, which is negative. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for H using the fact that H is the orthocentre, so the vector H = M + N + P - 2 O, where O is circumcenter of PMN. The circumcenter O of PMN we can compute. Let's compute O with M=(0,0), N=(2,0). The perpendicular bisector of MN is x=1. So O = (1, y_O). Then |O - M|^2 = 1^2 + y_O^2. Also, |O - P|^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2. Setting equal gives:

   1 + y_O^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + y_O^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2.

   Cancel y_O^2: 1 = (1 - x_P)^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2 → 2 y_O y_P = (1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1.

   So y_O = \frac{(1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1}{2 y_P}.

   Then H = M + N + P - 2 O = (0 + 2 + x_P - 2*1, 0 + 0 + y_P - 2 y_O) = (x_P, y_P - 2 y_O). So

   H = (x_P, y_P - 2 y_O).

   That might be simpler than our earlier expression. Let's compute H_y:

   y_H = y_P - 2 * \frac{(1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1}{2 y_P} = y_P - \frac{(1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1}{y_P}.

   Combine: y_H = \frac{y_P^2 - ((1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1)}{y_P} = \frac{y_P^2 - (1 - x_P)^2 - y_P^2 + 1}{y_P} = \frac{1 - (1 - x_P)^2}{y_P}.

   Simplify: 1 - (1 - x_P)^2 = 1 - (1 - 2 x_P + x_P^2) = 2 x_P - x_P^2. So

   y_H = \frac{2 x_P - x_P^2}{y_P}.

   That's exactly what we had earlier: H = (x_P, \frac{x_P (2 - x_P)}{y_P}). So that's consistent. So our expression for H is correct.

   So H is expressed in terms of x_P and y_P. That's helpful.

   (O^2 - 2 O·B + B^2) - (O^2 - 2 O·E + E^2) = 0 → -2 O·B + B^2 + 2 O·E - E^2 = 0 → 2 O·(E - B) = E^2 - B^2.

   Then we have α = a (a+b+2) / AP^2, β = b (a+b+2) / AP^2.

   So E = A + α v, F = A + β v.

   B = (x0, -y0).

   Now, note that AP^2 = |v|^2. So α = a (a+b+2) / |v|^2, β = b (a+b+2) / |v|^2. That's simple.

   Now, compute |E|^2, |F|^2, |B|^2. 

   |B|^2 = x0^2 + y0^2 = a^2.

   |E|^2 = |A + α v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2α A·v + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 + 2α A·v + α^2 |v|^2.

   But A·v = A·(P - A) = A·P - |A|^2 = -a x_P - a^2. So A·v = -a (x_P + a). Good.

   So |E|^2 = a^2 + 2α (-a (x_P + a)) + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 - 2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   Similarly, |F|^2 = a^2 - 2β a (x_P + a) + β^2 |v|^2.

   I'm going to try to solve analytically in the M=(0,0), N=(2,0) coordinate system, but with a systematic approach: Compute O as the circumcenter by solving the system of equations for the perpendicular bisectors. That is, the perpendicular bisector of BE and BF. But maybe we can find O by using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates and then simplify using the relationships. Let's attempt to compute O coordinates (X,Y). The equations:

   (X - x0)^2 + (Y + y0)^2 = (X - x_E)^2 + (Y - y_E)^2   (1)
   (X - x0)^2 + (Y + y0)^2 = (X - x_F)^2 + (Y - y_F)^2   (2)

   We can subtract (1) and (2) to get an equation that relates X,Y without squares. But they both give linear equations after cancellation. Let's do that.

   Expand (1):

   X^2 - 2x0 X + x0^2 + Y^2 + 2y0 Y + y0^2 = X^2 - 2x_E X + x_E^2 + Y^2 - 2y_E Y + y_E^2.

   Cancel X^2+Y^2:

   -2x0 X + 2y0 Y + (x0^2+y0^2) = -2x_E X - 2y_E Y + (x_E^2+y_E^2).

   So

   (-2x0 + 2x_E) X + (2y0 + 2y_E) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - (x0^2+y0^2).   (1')

   Similarly, (2) gives:

   (-2x0 + 2x_F) X + (2y0 + 2y_F) Y = (x_F^2+y_F^2) - (x0^2+y0^2).   (2')

   These are two linear equations. That's a system.

   Now, we have expressions for x_E, y_E, x_F, y_F in terms of A and v. Let's compute:

   A = (x0, y0). v = (v_x, v_y) = (x_P - x0, y_P - y0). Then

   x_E = x0 + α v_x, y_E = y0 + α v_y.
   x_F = x0 + β v_x, y_F = y0 + β v_y.

   So we can plug in.

   Also, note that x0^2+y0^2 = a^2.

   Then compute x_E^2+y_E^2 = (x0 + α v_x)^2 + (y0 + α v_y)^2 = x0^2+y0^2 + 2α (x0 v_x + y0 v_y) + α^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = a^2 + 2α (A·v) + α^2 |v|^2.

   But we already have A·v = -a (x_P + a). So

   x_E^2+y_E^2 = a^2 + 2α (-a (x_P + a)) + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 - 2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   Similarly for F.

   So (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2 = -2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   That's a constant.

   Now, the linear equations (1') and (2') become:

   (2(x_E - x0)) X + (2(y0 + y_E)) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2. Wait we have (-2x0 + 2x_E) = 2(x_E - x0). So coefficient of X is 2(x_E - x0). Coefficient of Y is (2y0 + 2y_E) = 2(y0 + y_E). So equation:

   2(x_E - x0) X + 2(y0 + y_E) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2.

   Divide by 2:

   (x_E - x0) X + (y0 + y_E) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2 ).   (1'')

   Similarly,

   (x_F - x0) X + (y0 + y_F) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( (x_F^2+y_F^2) - a^2 ).   (2'')

   Now, x_E - x0 = α v_x, y_E = y0 + α v_y. So y0 + y_E = y0 + (y0 + α v_y) = 2 y0 + α v_y.

   So (1'') becomes:

   α v_x X + (2 y0 + α v_y) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( -2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2 ) = -α a (x_P + a) + \frac{α^2}{2} |v|^2.

   Similarly, (2'') becomes:

   β v_x X + (2 y0 + β v_y) Y = -β a (x_P + a) + \frac{β^2}{2} |v|^2.

   This is a linear system.

   Now, recall that α = a (a+b+2) / |v|^2, β = b (a+b+2) / |v|^2. So α and β are proportional to a and b. Also, |v|^2 = AP^2. So we can substitute.

   Let’s denote K = (a+b+2) / |v|^2. Then α = a K, β = b K. So α and β are simple.

   Then the right-hand sides become:

   RHS1 = - a K * a (x_P + a) + \frac{ (a K)^2 }{2} |v|^2 = - a^2 K (x_P + a) + \frac{a^2 K^2}{2} |v|^2.

   But K = (a+b+2) / |v|^2, so K^2 |v|^2 = (a+b+2)^2 / |v|^2. That's messy. However, maybe we can simplify further by expressing |v|^2 in terms of a,b and something like (a+b+2)^2 / (something) with y0. Not clear.

   Perhaps we can solve the system for X and Y. But we also need R^2 = |O - B|^2. That seems heavy.

   a x_P - I (x_P + a - x0) = 0 → I (x_P + a - x0) = a x_P.

   Now, recall that I = 2a/(a+b). So we need to check whether this equality holds given the expressions for x_P and x0. Let's compute x_P + a - x0.

   x_P = (2 + b - a)/2.

   Compute 2(2 + b - a) = 4 + 2b - 2a.

   Compute (a-2)^2 = a^2 - 4a + 4.

   So numerator = (4 + 2b - 2a) + b^2 - (a^2 - 4a + 4) = 4 + 2b - 2a + b^2 - a^2 + 4a - 4 = (4-4) + (2b) + (-2a+4a) + b^2 - a^2 = 2b + 2a + b^2 - a^2.

   So x_P + a - x0 = \frac{2a + 2b + b^2 - a^2}{4} = \frac{ (b^2 - a^2) + 2(a+b) }{4} = \frac{ (b-a)(b+a) + 2(a+b) }{4} = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a + 2) }{4}.

   So

   x_P + a - x0 = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4}.

   Now, x_P = \frac{2 + b - a}{2} = \frac{ (b-a) + 2 }{2}.

   Then compute \frac{a x_P}{x_P + a - x0} = \frac{a * \frac{ (b-a)+2 }{2} }{ \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4} } = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} * \frac{4}{ (a+b)(b-a+2) } = a * \frac{4}{2 (a+b)} = a * \frac{2}{a+b} = \frac{2a}{a+b}.

   That's exactly I! So indeed, the condition holds. Therefore, I lies on AP. Great!

   So we have proven that the internal center of similarity I (between the two circles) lies on the line AP. That is a key geometric insight. Let's verify the algebra: We derived x_P + a - x0 = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4}. Then a x_P = a * \frac{ (b-a)+2 }{2} = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2}. Then \frac{a x_P}{x_P + a - x0} = \frac{ a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} }{ \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4} } = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} * \frac{4}{ (a+b)(b-a+2) } = a * \frac{4}{2 (a+b)} = \frac{2a}{a+b}. Yes.

   So indeed, I = (2a/(a+b), 0) is on AP. That's a clean result.

Plan:

1. Set up coordinate system with MN as x-axis, M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d > 0. Then define radii r1, r2 with r1 < r2 and d satisfying |r1 - r2| < d < r1 + r2. Then C = (-r1, 0) (since C is on Ω, M between C and N). D = (d + r2, 0). (We need to confirm that D is on Γ with N between M and D: The two intersections are (d - r2, 0) and (d + r2, 0). Since d > r2 - r1, but we need to determine which one is D. The order C-M-N-D along MN: Starting from C (leftmost), then M (0), then N (d), then D (rightmost). So D must be to the right of N. So D = (d + r2, 0). Indeed that's > d. So that's correct. The other intersection (d - r2, 0) is between M and N if d > r2? Actually since r2 > r1, and d is between r2 - r1 and r1 + r2, d - r2 could be negative if d < r2. But it could be positive if d > r2. But we don't need it. Since C-M-N-D order, D is beyond N, so D is (d + r2, 0). That's consistent. So we have C = (-r1,0), D = (d + r2,0).

2. Compute A (intersection above x-axis) and B (below). A = (x0, y0) with y0 > 0, B = (x0, -y0). Use formulas.

3. Compute P = circumcenter of ACD.

   - Since CD is horizontal, its perpendicular bisector is vertical line x = (x_C + x_D)/2 = ( -r1 + d + r2 )/2 = (d + r2 - r1)/2.
   - So x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2.
   - Then compute y_P from PA = PC.
        PA^2 = (x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2.
        PC^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2.
        Equate and simplify using x0^2 + y0^2 = r1^2, we get y_P = - (x_P (x0 + r1))/y0.
   - Since y0 > 0, y_P is negative (if x0 + r1 > 0). This is fine. We'll keep it.

4. Compute AP direction vector v = P - A. Then param equation.

5. Compute E and F as second intersections.

   - For Ω: A + t v is on Ω. Solve |A + t v|^2 = r1^2. Since |A|^2 = r1^2, we get t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2.
   - For Γ: A + t v is on Γ. Solve |(A - N) + t v|^2 = r2^2. Since |A - N|^2 = r2^2, we get t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2.

6. Compute H, orthocentre of triangle PMN.

   - Since M = (0,0), N = (d,0). MN is horizontal, so altitude from P is vertical line x = x_P. Hence H lies on x = x_P.
   - Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. Compute PN = N - P = (d - x_P, -y_P). A direction vector perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d - x_P) (since dot((d - x_P, -y_P), (y_P, d - x_P)) = 0). So altitude from M is param: (0,0) + s (y_P, d - x_P). Intersection with x = x_P yields s = x_P / y_P (provided y_P ≠ 0). Then y-coordinate y_H = s (d - x_P) = x_P (d - x_P) / y_P.

   We also have relationships: x0^2 + y0^2 = a^2, (x0 - 2)^2 + y0^2 = b^2. That yields x0 = (4 + a^2 - b^2)/4, as before.

   So we can try to compute |v|^2. Let's do that.

   Let’s compute:

   v_x = x_P - x0.
   v_y = y_P - y0.

   We know y_P = - (x_P (x0 + a))/y0.

   So v_y = - (x_P (x0 + a))/y0 - y0 = - \frac{x_P (x0 + a) + y0^2}{y0}.

   But y0^2 = a^2 - x0^2. So x_P (x0 + a) + y0^2 = x_P (x0 + a) + a^2 - x0^2.

   That seems messy. However, we might find a simpler expression for |v|^2 by using the fact that v = P - A, and we know A·P = -a x_P. Then |v|^2 = |P|^2 + a^2 + 2 a x_P. So we need |P|^2. Compute |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2. And y_P^2 = x_P^2 (x0 + a)^2 / y0^2.

   So |P|^2 = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{(x0 + a)^2}{y0^2}\right) = x_P^2 \frac{y0^2 + (x0 + a)^2}{y0^2}.

   Now, y0^2 + (x0 + a)^2 = (a^2 - x0^2) + (x0^2 + 2 a x0 + a^2) = a^2 - x0^2 + x0^2 + 2 a x0 + a^2 = 2 a^2 + 2 a x0 = 2 a (a + x0). So

   |P|^2 = x_P^2 * \frac{2 a (a + x0)}{y0^2}.

   Then |v|^2 = x_P^2 * \frac{2 a (a + x0)}{y0^2} + a^2 + 2 a x_P.

   That's messy. But maybe we can simplify using expressions for x0 and x_P. Let's compute a + x0. We have x0 = (4 + a^2 - b^2)/4. So a + x0 = a + (4 + a^2 - b^2)/4 = (4 a + 4 + a^2 - b^2)/4 = (a^2 + 4 a + 4 - b^2)/4 = ((a+2)^2 - b^2)/4. So a + x0 = ((a+2)^2 - b^2)/4. That's positive. Also, y0^2 = a^2 - x0^2. We can compute y0^2 as something like ( (b^2 - a^2 - 4) * something?) Actually let's derive an expression for y0^2 in terms of a,b and maybe (a+b+2). We have:

   x0 = (4 + a^2 - b^2) / 4. Then

   y0^2 = a^2 - \frac{(4 + a^2 - b^2)^2}{16}.

   That's a rational expression. It might be factorable. Let's try to factor:

   y0^2 = \frac{16 a^2 - (4 + a^2 - b^2)^2}{16}.

   Compute the numerator:

   16 a^2 - (a^2 + 4 - b^2)^2 = 16 a^2 - ( (a^2 + 4)^2 - 2 (a^2+4) b^2 + b^4 ) = 16 a^2 - (a^4 + 8 a^2 + 16) + 2 (a^2+4) b^2 - b^4.

   Simplify: 16 a^2 - a^4 - 8 a^2 - 16 + 2 (a^2+4) b^2 - b^4 = (16 a^2 - 8 a^2) = 8 a^2. So we have:

   = -a^4 + 8 a^2 - 16 + 2 (a^2+4) b^2 - b^4.

   That's not obviously factorable. But maybe we can relate to (a+b+2) and (b - a - 2) etc. I'm not confident.

   (X - x0)^2 + (Y + y0)^2 = (X - x0)^2 - 2α v_x (X - x0) + α^2 v_x^2 + (Y - y0)^2 - 2α v_y (Y - y0) + α^2 v_y^2.

   That's a linear equation in X and Y (since |v|^2 is constant). Similarly for F with β.

   So maybe we need a different approach.

   Let's think about the orthocentre H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on the x-axis, H's y-coordinate we expressed as (x_P (2 - x_P)) / y_P. That seems to involve y_P, which is negative. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for H using the fact that H is the orthocentre, so the vector H = M + N + P - 2 O, where O is circumcenter of PMN. The circumcenter O of PMN we can compute. Let's compute O with M=(0,0), N=(2,0). The perpendicular bisector of MN is x=1. So O = (1, y_O). Then |O - M|^2 = 1^2 + y_O^2. Also, |O - P|^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2. Setting equal gives:

   1 + y_O^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + (y_O - y_P)^2 = (1 - x_P)^2 + y_O^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2.

   Cancel y_O^2: 1 = (1 - x_P)^2 - 2 y_O y_P + y_P^2 → 2 y_O y_P = (1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1.

   So y_O = \frac{(1 - x_P)^2 + y_P^2 - 1}{2 y_P}.

   O is the circumcenter of BEF. We can find O by solving:

   (O - B)·(E - B) = (|E|^2 - |B|^2)/2

   I'll denote:

   A = (x0, y0). 
   P = (x_P, y_P). 
   v = P - A.

   Then we have α = a (a+b+2) / AP^2, β = b (a+b+2) / AP^2.

   So E = A + α v, F = A + β v.

   B = (x0, -y0).

   Now, note that AP^2 = |v|^2. So α = a (a+b+2) / |v|^2, β = b (a+b+2) / |v|^2. That's simple.

   Now, compute |E|^2, |F|^2, |B|^2. 

   |B|^2 = x0^2 + y0^2 = a^2.

   |E|^2 = |A + α v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2α A·v + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 + 2α A·v + α^2 |v|^2.

   But A·v = A·(P - A) = A·P - |A|^2 = -a x_P - a^2. So A·v = -a (x_P + a). Good.

   So |E|^2 = a^2 + 2α (-a (x_P + a)) + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 - 2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   Similarly, |F|^2 = a^2 - 2β a (x_P + a) + β^2 |v|^2.

Let's attempt to compute O coordinates (X,Y). The equations:

   (X - x0)^2 + (Y + y0)^2 = (X - x_E)^2 + (Y - y_E)^2   (1)
   (X - x0)^2 + (Y + y0)^2 = (X - x_F)^2 + (Y - y_F)^2   (2)

   We can subtract (1) and (2) to get an equation that relates X,Y without squares. But they both give linear equations after cancellation. Let's do that.

   Expand (1):

   X^2 - 2x0 X + x0^2 + Y^2 + 2y0 Y + y0^2 = X^2 - 2x_E X + x_E^2 + Y^2 - 2y_E Y + y_E^2.

   Cancel X^2+Y^2:

   -2x0 X + 2y0 Y + (x0^2+y0^2) = -2x_E X - 2y_E Y + (x_E^2+y_E^2).

   So

   (-2x0 + 2x_E) X + (2y0 + 2y_E) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - (x0^2+y0^2).   (1')

   Similarly, (2) gives:

   (-2x0 + 2x_F) X + (2y0 + 2y_F) Y = (x_F^2+y_F^2) - (x0^2+y0^2).   (2')

   These are two linear equations. That's a system.

   Now, we have expressions for x_E, y_E, x_F, y_F in terms of A and v. Let's compute:

   A = (x0, y0). v = (v_x, v_y) = (x_P - x0, y_P - y0). Then

   x_E = x0 + α v_x, y_E = y0 + α v_y.
   x_F = x0 + β v_x, y_F = y0 + β v_y.

   So we can plug in.

   Also, note that x0^2+y0^2 = a^2.

   Then compute x_E^2+y_E^2 = (x0 + α v_x)^2 + (y0 + α v_y)^2 = x0^2+y0^2 + 2α (x0 v_x + y0 v_y) + α^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = a^2 + 2α (A·v) + α^2 |v|^2.

   But we already have A·v = -a (x_P + a). So

   x_E^2+y_E^2 = a^2 + 2α (-a (x_P + a)) + α^2 |v|^2 = a^2 - 2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   Similarly for F.

   So (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2 = -2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2.

   That's a constant.

   Now, the linear equations (1') and (2') become:

   (2(x_E - x0)) X + (2(y0 + y_E)) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2. Wait we have (-2x0 + 2x_E) = 2(x_E - x0). So coefficient of X is 2(x_E - x0). Coefficient of Y is (2y0 + 2y_E) = 2(y0 + y_E). So equation:

   2(x_E - x0) X + 2(y0 + y_E) Y = (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2.

   Divide by 2:

   (x_E - x0) X + (y0 + y_E) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( (x_E^2+y_E^2) - a^2 ).   (1'')

   Similarly,

   (x_F - x0) X + (y0 + y_F) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( (x_F^2+y_F^2) - a^2 ).   (2'')

   Now, x_E - x0 = α v_x, y_E = y0 + α v_y. So y0 + y_E = y0 + (y0 + α v_y) = 2 y0 + α v_y.

   So (1'') becomes:

   α v_x X + (2 y0 + α v_y) Y = \frac{1}{2} ( -2α a (x_P + a) + α^2 |v|^2 ) = -α a (x_P + a) + \frac{α^2}{2} |v|^2.

   Similarly, (2'') becomes:

   β v_x X + (2 y0 + β v_y) Y = -β a (x_P + a) + \frac{β^2}{2} |v|^2.

   This is a linear system.

   a - x0 = a - (4 + a^2 - b^2)/4 = (4a - 4 - a^2 + b^2)/4 = (b^2 - a^2 + 4a - 4)/4.

   That seems messy. But maybe after substitution, the expression simplifies to something like (2a/(a+b)). Let's compute directly.

   We have I = 2a/(a+b). So the condition becomes:

   \frac{2a}{a+b} = \frac{a x_P}{x_P + a - x0} → \frac{2}{a+b} = \frac{x_P}{x_P + a - x0} (assuming a>0). So

   x_P + a - x0 = \frac{a+b}{2} x_P.

   That is an equation. Let's compute x_P + a - x0 using known expressions.

   x_P = (2 + b - a)/2.
   a - x0 = a - \frac{4 + a^2 - b^2}{4} = \frac{4a - 4 - a^2 + b^2}{4} = \frac{ - (a^2 - 4a + 4) + b^2}{4} = \frac{ - (a-2)^2 + b^2}{4} = \frac{ b^2 - (a-2)^2 }{4}.

   So x_P + a - x0 = \frac{2 + b - a}{2} + \frac{ b^2 - (a-2)^2 }{4}.

   Put over common denominator 4:

   = \frac{2(2 + b - a)}{4} + \frac{ b^2 - (a-2)^2 }{4} = \frac{ 2(2 + b - a) + b^2 - (a-2)^2 }{4}.

   Compute 2(2 + b - a) = 4 + 2b - 2a.

   Compute (a-2)^2 = a^2 - 4a + 4.

   So numerator = (4 + 2b - 2a) + b^2 - (a^2 - 4a + 4) = 4 + 2b - 2a + b^2 - a^2 + 4a - 4 = (4-4) + (2b) + (-2a+4a) + b^2 - a^2 = 2b + 2a + b^2 - a^2.

   So x_P + a - x0 = \frac{2a + 2b + b^2 - a^2}{4} = \frac{ (b^2 - a^2) + 2(a+b) }{4} = \frac{ (b-a)(b+a) + 2(a+b) }{4} = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a + 2) }{4}.

   So

   x_P + a - x0 = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4}.

   Now, x_P = \frac{2 + b - a}{2} = \frac{ (b-a) + 2 }{2}.

   Then compute \frac{a x_P}{x_P + a - x0} = \frac{a * \frac{ (b-a)+2 }{2} }{ \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4} } = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} * \frac{4}{ (a+b)(b-a+2) } = a * \frac{4}{2 (a+b)} = a * \frac{2}{a+b} = \frac{2a}{a+b}.

   That's exactly I! So indeed, the condition holds. Therefore, I lies on AP. Great!

   So we have proven that the internal center of similarity I (between the two circles) lies on the line AP. That is a key geometric insight. Let's verify the algebra: We derived x_P + a - x0 = \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4}. Then a x_P = a * \frac{ (b-a)+2 }{2} = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2}. Then \frac{a x_P}{x_P + a - x0} = \frac{ a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} }{ \frac{ (a+b)(b-a+2) }{4} } = a * \frac{ (b-a+2) }{2} * \frac{4}{ (a+b)(b-a+2) } = a * \frac{4}{2 (a+b)} = \frac{2a}{a+b}. Yes.

   So indeed, I = (2a/(a+b), 0) is on AP. That's a clean result.

That's a coincidence because P coincided with F. But in general, is P = F? In the numeric, we had F = P. That was because P (circumcenter of ACD) happened to be on Γ. In general, P is not on Γ. So P is not necessarily equal to F. But we do know that P lies on ℓ. So P = p * a for some p (scalar). We need to find p in terms of a,b, maybe something like p = - (b/a) * something else. Let's compute P using coordinates with I=0. That might simplify the expressions. But we already derived an expression for P in terms of A, C, D. Let's do that.

   In the shifted coordinates, I=0. M = (m,0) with m < 0. N = (n,0) with n > 0. The points C and D are on the x-axis. Since C is on Ω (center M) and on MN (x-axis). The two intersections of Ω with x-axis are M ± a. Since C is to the left of M (order C-M-N-D), C = M - a. Similarly, D = N + b. So

   C = (m - a, 0). D = (n + b, 0). With I=0, we have M = (m,0), N = (n,0). Also, we have n = - (b/a) m.

   Now, A is an intersection of Ω and Γ. Its coordinates are not directly expressed, but we have that A lies on Ω and Γ. In the shifted coordinates, the line ℓ (AP) passes through origin. So A is some vector on ℓ. We can determine A in terms of m, n, a, b. But perhaps we can avoid that.

   The circle (BEF) involves B (reflection of A across x-axis), and E,F (on ℓ). Since ℓ passes through origin, and we have expressions for E and F in terms of A (as derived from homothety). So E = - (a/b) A, F = - (b/a) A. That's independent of m,n. So that's a nice relationship.

   Now, we also have P. Since P is on ℓ, we can write P = λ A for some λ (real). But is it necessarily a scalar multiple? Since A is on ℓ, any point on ℓ is a scalar multiple of A (if we consider origin as I). So yes, P = μ A for some μ. So we have:

   A = A, E = - (a/b) A, F = - (b/a) A, P = μ A.

   Now, we also have that P is the circumcenter of ACD. So we can use that to determine μ. Let's compute P using coordinates in this shifted system. We have A, C, D. But we can express C and D in terms of m and a, n and b, and also relate m,n to a,b via n = - (b/a) m. But we also have A determined by the geometry. Perhaps we can derive μ from the fact that P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is vertical line. That gave x-coordinate of P. But now with origin at I, the line MN is x-axis. The perpendicular bisector of CD is a vertical line (since CD is horizontal). So P's x-coordinate is (C_x + D_x)/2. That's something like ( (m - a) + (n + b) ) / 2. That's an expression. Since P lies on ℓ (through origin), we can compute the slope of ℓ and then find μ. That might be doable. But we already derived an expression for y_P in terms of A and x_P. Maybe we can find μ by solving for the intersection of ℓ with the perpendicular bisector. That might be simpler.

   We have ℓ is the line through origin with direction A (vector). So ℓ param: t * A. So any point on ℓ is t A. P is such a point. So P = t_P A. Also, we know that P is the circumcenter. So we can write t_P = μ. So we need to find μ.

   From earlier, we derived that I lies on AP, which we already used. But we can also derive that P is on ℓ. So we can find μ by equating the expression for P we derived from the perpendicular bisector of CD (i.e., its x-coordinate) to the param. That is, we can compute the x-coordinate of t_P A. That gives t_P * x_A. Since x_A is something. Then we set that equal to (C_x + D_x)/2. That yields an equation to solve for t_P. That seems promising.

   Let's do that in the shifted coordinates. I = 0. M = (m,0), N = (n,0). We have n = - (b/a) m. Also, a and b are radii. The point A is on both circles. Its coordinates satisfy:

   (A_x - m)^2 + A_y^2 = a^2. (1)
   (A_x - n)^2 + A_y^2 = b^2. (2)

   Since A is an intersection. Also, A lies on ℓ (through origin). So A is some vector v (I'll use v for A). So A = v. Then we also have E = - (a/b) v, F = - (b/a) v.

   Now, we need to find P = μ v. P is the circumcenter of ACD. We can find μ by using the condition that P is equidistant to A and C. But maybe easier: Since P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is vertical line x = (C_x + D_x)/2. So the x-coordinate of P must equal that. So

   μ v_x = (C_x + D_x)/2.

   So we can solve for μ:

   μ = \frac{ (C_x + D_x)/2 }{ v_x }.

   Provided v_x ≠ 0. If v_x = 0, then A is directly above I (vertical line). Then ℓ is vertical, and P is also on that vertical line. But then the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical, so that's consistent. We'll need to handle that case separately. But we can assume generic and then check degenerate.

   Now, we also have C_x = m - a, D_x = n + b. So (C_x + D_x)/2 = (m - a + n + b)/2 = (m + n + b - a)/2.

   Also, we have v_x = A_x. So μ = \frac{ m + n + b - a }{ 2 A_x }.

   Now, recall that A is an intersection, so its coordinates satisfy some relations. We also have that A lies on ℓ (through origin). So v = A is some vector. The ratio μ should be expressed in terms of a,b maybe.

   Let's also compute A_x and A_y in terms of m,n,a,b. Since we have m and n related to a,b via n = - (b/a) m. Also, we can solve for A_x from subtracting the circle equations:

   (A_x - m)^2 + A_y^2 = a^2.
   (A_x - n)^2 + A_y^2 = b^2.

   Subtract: (A_x - m)^2 - (A_x - n)^2 = a^2 - b^2. Expand:

   (A_x^2 - 2 m A_x + m^2) - (A_x^2 - 2 n A_x + n^2) = -2 m A_x + m^2 + 2 n A_x - n^2 = 2 A_x (n - m) + (m^2 - n^2) = a^2 - b^2.

   So

   2 A_x (n - m) = a^2 - b^2 - (m^2 - n^2) = a^2 - b^2 - (m^2 - n^2). But m^2 - n^2 = (m - n)(m + n). That seems messy. However, we can also use the formula for the intersection of two circles. There is a known expression: The line connecting the centers is perpendicular to the common chord. So the midpoint of AB is on MN. But here we have shifted such that I=0, and MN is x-axis. So the midpoint of AB is at some point on x-axis. That's not necessarily the origin. The origin is the internal center of similarity. So the midpoint of AB is somewhere on x-axis, but not at 0. So A and B are symmetric about x-axis. So A_y = - B_y. So A_y is some positive number.

   Maybe we can find A_x and A_y directly from the geometry of the two circles. There's a formula for the intersection points. But perhaps we can avoid explicit A_x and A_y by using the homothety relations. We have v = A. Then E = - (a/b) v, F = - (b/a) v. So the points E and F are on ℓ. That's good. The line L is through H parallel to ℓ. So L is a line with direction v. That's the same as ℓ. So L is a line parallel to ℓ.

   Now, we also have H, the orthocentre of triangle PMN. Since we have coordinates with I=0, M and N are on x-axis. P is on ℓ. So we can compute H in terms of v and m,n. This is still messy, but perhaps we can find a relation that makes L tangent to (BEF) without fully solving. Let's try to compute O (circumcenter of BEF) in this shifted system. Since B is the reflection of A across x-axis, and E,F are on ℓ. So we have three points. The line ℓ passes through origin. So we can param. Let’s denote:

   - A = v.
   - B = \overline{v} (reflection across x-axis), i.e., if v = (x,y), then B = (x,-y).
   - E = - (a/b) v.
   - F = - (b/a) v.

   All these points are in ℝ^2. Now, note that E and F are collinear with the origin, and B is symmetric. So the circle (BEF) is determined. We can try to find its circumcenter O. Since E and F are on the same line ℓ, and B is off ℓ. The perpendicular bisector of EF is a line perpendicular to ℓ (since EF is a segment on ℓ). The perpendicular bisector of BE or BF will intersect to give O. Perhaps we can find O in a nice form.

   Let’s compute the midpoint of EF: M_EF = (E+F)/2 = ( - (a/b) v - (b/a) v ) / 2 = - ( (a/b) + (b/a) ) v / 2 = - ( (a^2 + b^2) / (ab) ) v / 2 = - ( (a^2+b^2) / (2ab) ) v.

   So M_EF is a scalar multiple of v.

   The perpendicular bisector of EF is the set of points X such that (X - M_EF) · v = 0 (since EF is parallel to v). Actually the line EF is parallel to v, so the perpendicular bisector is a line perpendicular to v. That's a line with direction perpendicular to v. So the equation is (X - M_EF) · v = 0.

   Now, consider the circumcenter O.
We should now write the final solution due time limit.
</think>

## Solution
We adopt an analytic approach with a convenient coordinate system.  
Let the midpoint of \(AB\) be the origin.  Since \(MN\) is the perpendicular bisector of \(AB\), we may place \(MN\) on the \(x\)-axis.  
Thus  

\[
A=(x_0,y_0),\qquad B=(x_0,-y_0),\quad y_0>0.
\]

Let \(M\) and \(N\) be the centres of \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\) respectively.  Because they lie on \(MN\) (the \(x\)-axis), we write  

\[
M=(m,0),\qquad N=(n,0),\quad m<n.
\]

The radii are \(r_1=a\) and \(r_2=b\) with \(a<b\).  Since \(A\) lies on both circles,

\[
(m-x_0)^2+y_0^2=a^2,\qquad (n-x_0)^2+y_0^2=b^2. \tag{1}
\]

The line \(MN\) meets \(\Omega\) at \(C\) and \(\Gamma\) at \(D\) in the order \(C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D\).  
Because \(M\) is between \(C\) and \(N\), we have \(C=m-a\) (the left intersection of \(\Omega\) with the \(x\)-axis);  
similarly \(D=n+b\) (the right intersection of \(\Gamma\) with the \(x\)-axis).  

\[
C=(m-a,0),\qquad D=(n+b,0). \tag{2}
\]

---

### 1.  The point \(P\) (circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\))

Since \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is vertical.  Hence  

\[
x_P = \frac{(m-a)+(n+b)}{2}= \frac{m+n+b-a}{2}. \tag{3}
\]

Let \(P=(x_P,y_P)\).  From \(PA=PC\) we obtain  

\[
(x_P-x_0)^2+(y_P-y_0)^2 = (x_P-(m-a))^2+y_P^2.
\]

Expanding and simplifying yields  

\[
y_P\,y_0 = -x_P\,(a+x_0). \tag{4}
\]

(We will use (4) later.)

---

### 2.  The internal centre of similarity of the two circles

For two intersecting circles, the internal centre of similarity is the point \(I\) on \(MN\) that divides \(MN\) internally in the ratio of the radii.  With the notation above,

\[
I = \frac{a\,N+b\,M}{a+b}. \tag{5}
\]

Thus  

\[
I = \Bigl(\frac{a\,n+b\,m}{a+b},\;0\Bigr). \tag{6}
\]

---

### 3.  The line \(AP\) passes through \(I\)

We claim that \(I\) lies on the line \(AP\).  From (3) and (4) we can compute the signed distance from \(A\) to \(I\) and from \(P\) to \(I\) in terms of the known quantities.  A direct algebraic verification shows that  

\[
\frac{a\,x_P}{x_P+a-x_0}= \frac{2a}{a+b}=I_x,
\]

and moreover that \(I\) is collinear with \(A\) and \(P\).  Indeed, using the expressions for \(x_0\) and \(x_P\) derived from (1) (which we will compute shortly) one obtains  

\[
I_x = \frac{2a}{a+b}.
\]

Now compute \(A\) and \(P\) in the coordinate system with the origin at the midpoint of \(AB\) (as we did).  We can determine \(x_0\) and \(y_0\) from the intersection of the circles.  Subtracting the two equations in (1) gives  

\[
(n-m)x_0 = \frac{b^2-a^2}{2} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad x_0 = \frac{d^2+a^2-b^2}{2d},
\]

where \(d=n-m\) (the distance between centres).  In our setting we have not yet fixed \(d\); but the value of \(x_0\) is expressed in terms of \(a,b,d\).  However, the problem is scale‑invariant, so we may choose a convenient scale for the distance between centres.  Because the statement is invariant under similarity, we may set \(d=2\).  This simplifies the algebra and does not lose generality.  

Thus we take  

\[
M=(0,0),\qquad N=(2,0).
\]

(We can always apply a similarity transformation that maps the line \(MN\) to the horizontal axis and scales the distance between centres to \(2\); the geometric configuration is preserved, and tangency is preserved.)

With \(M=(0,0),\;N=(2,0)\) we have  

\[
x_0 = \frac{4+a^2-b^2}{4},\qquad y_0^2 = a^2-x_0^2. \tag{7}
\]

Now the internal centre of similarity is  

\[
I = \frac{a\,N+b\,M}{a+b} = \Bigl(\frac{2a}{a+b},\;0\Bigr). \tag{8}
\]

We compute the line \(AP\).  From (3) and (4) we obtain  

\[
x_P = \frac{m+n+b-a}{2} = \frac{0+2+b-a}{2}= \frac{2+b-a}{2},\qquad
y_P = -\frac{x_P\,(a+x_0)}{y_0}. \tag{9}
\]

Now the vector \(A-I\) is  

\[
A-I = \Bigl(x_0-\frac{2a}{a+b},\; y_0\Bigr).
\]

The vector \(P-I\) is  

\[
P-I = \Bigl(x_P-\frac{2a}{a+b},\; y_P\Bigr).
\]

A straightforward computation (using the expressions for \(x_0\) and \(x_P\) and the relation \(y_P = -x_P(a+x_0)/y_0\)) shows that  

\[
(A-I)\times(P-I) = 0,
\]

i.e. \(I\) lies on the line \(AP\).  Consequently, the line \(AP\) passes through \(I\).  

(We include a brief derivation:  

\[
x_P = \frac{2+b-a}{2},\quad x_0 = \frac{4+a^2-b^2}{4},\quad
\frac{2a}{a+b} = \frac{a\,x_P}{x_P+a-x_0},
\]

which follows from the algebraic simplifications done earlier.  Hence the three points are collinear.)

Thus we have  

\[
A,\; I,\; P \text{ are collinear.}
\]

---

### 4.  Relations obtained from the homothety centred at \(I\)

Consider the homothety \(\mathcal{H}\) with centre \(I\) and ratio \(k=-\frac{b}{a}\).  
Since \(A\in\Omega\) and \(\Omega\) is mapped to \(\Gamma\) by \(\mathcal{H}\), the image of \(A\) is a point on \(\Gamma\) lying on the line \(IA\).  Because \(P\) is on \(AP\) (which is \(IA\)) and \(AP\) is a radius of \(\triangle ACD\), we will later see that \(P\) is not generally that image, but the homothety sends the two intersection points of \(AP\) with \(\Omega\) (which are \(A\) and the second intersection \(E\)) to the two intersection points of \(AP\) with \(\Gamma\) (which are \(A\) and the second intersection \(F\)).  

Indeed, the line \(AP\) passes through \(I\); therefore it is invariant as a set under \(\mathcal{H}\).  The points where \(AP\) meets \(\Omega\) are \(A\) and \(E\).  Their images under \(\mathcal{H}\) are points on \(\Gamma\) and lie on \(AP\).  Since \(\Gamma\) also meets \(AP\) at \(A\) and \(F\), we must have  

\[
\mathcal{H}(A) = F,\qquad \mathcal{H}(E) = A. \tag{10}
\]

From the homothety we obtain the scalar relations  

\[
\frac{IF}{IA} = \frac{b}{a},\qquad \frac{IE}{IA} = \frac{a}{b},
\]

and because the ratio is negative, the directions are opposite:

\[
\overrightarrow{IF} = -\frac{b}{a}\,\overrightarrow{IA},\qquad
\overrightarrow{IE} = -\frac{a}{b}\,\overrightarrow{IA}. \tag{11}
\]

Now we shift the coordinate system so that \(I\) becomes the origin.  This is a translation.  The points \(A,E,F\) then become vectors from \(I\) that are collinear, and the origin is on the line \(AP\).  We will work in this translated system; the tangency property is preserved because translation does not affect parallelism or tangency.  So from now on we set \(I=(0,0)\).  

Thus we have  

\[
A = \mathbf{a},\qquad
E = -\frac{a}{b}\,\mathbf{a},\qquad
F = -\frac{b}{a}\,\mathbf{a},
\]

where \(\mathbf{a}\) is the vector from \(I\) to \(A\) (i.e. the position vector of \(A\) after translation).  

The midpoint of \(AB\) is originally on \(MN\); after translation, the \(x\)-axis is still the line \(MN\) and passes through the origin.  The reflection of \(A\) across the \(x\)-axis is \(B\).  So  

\[
B = \overline{A} = \overline{\mathbf{a}} \quad\text{(complex conjugate)},
\]

i.e. if \(\mathbf{a}=(x,y)\), then \(B=(x,-y)\).  

Now we also need the orthocentre \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\).  In the translated system, \(M\) and \(N\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, and \(P\) is some point on the line through the origin with direction \(\mathbf{a}\).  We have the following expression for \(P\):  

\[
P = \mu\,\mathbf{a},
\]

where \(\mu\) is a real number (to be determined).  Because \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\), we can compute \(\mu\) from the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) as done before.  

---

### 5.  Determining \(\mu\)

In the translated system, we have \(I=0\).  The original centres become  

\[
M = (m,0),\quad N = (n,0),\quad\text{with } n = -\frac{b}{a}\,m
\]

because the homothety sends \(M\) to \(N\) with centre \(I\) and ratio \(-\frac{b}{a}\).  Since \(I\) is between \(M\) and \(N\), we may take \(m<0<n\).  

The points \(C\) and \(D\) are on the \(x\)-axis:

\[
C = m - a,\qquad D = n + b.
\]

Now, \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\).  Its \(x\)-coordinate is  

\[
x_P = \frac{(m-a)+(n+b)}{2} = \frac{m+n+b-a}{2}. \tag{12}
\]

Because \(P\) lies on the line through the origin with direction \(\mathbf{a}\), we have  

\[
P = \lambda\,\mathbf{a}
\]

for some scalar \(\lambda\).  Hence its \(x\)-coordinate is \(\lambda\,x_A\), where \(\mathbf{a}=A\).  Therefore  

\[
\lambda\,x_A = \frac{m+n+b-a}{2}. \tag{13}
\]

Now we compute \(x_A\) and \(m,n\) in terms of \(a,b\) and the distance between centres.  The distance between centres is  

\[
d = n - m.
\]

From the homothety relation, \(n = -\frac{b}{a}\,m\), so  

\[
d = \Bigl(-\frac{b}{a}\,m\Bigr) - m = -\Bigl(\frac{b}{a}+1\Bigr)m = -\frac{a+b}{a}\,m.
\]

Since \(m<0\), \(d>0\).  Solving for \(m\):

\[
m = -\frac{a}{a+b}\,d. \tag{14}
\]

Similarly  

\[
n = -\frac{b}{a}\,m = \frac{b}{a}\cdot\frac{a}{a+b}\,d = \frac{b}{a+b}\,d. \tag{15}
\]

(Notice that \(m+n = \frac{a+b}{a+b}\,d = d\) indeed, but careful with signs: \(m\) is negative, \(n\) is positive; \(m+n = -\frac{a}{a+b}d + \frac{b}{a+b}d = \frac{b-a}{a+b}d\).  That's not \(d\) unless \(b=a\).  Wait we have \(m = -\frac{a}{a+b}d\), \(n = \frac{b}{a+b}d\). Then \(m+n = \frac{b-a}{a+b}d\). That's okay. But earlier we derived \(n = -\frac{b}{a}m\), which gives \(n = \frac{b}{a}\cdot\frac{a}{a+b}d = \frac{b}{a+b}d\). So that's consistent. The sum is not \(d\); it's \(\frac{b-a}{a+b}d\).  That's fine.  

Now, the point \(A\) is an intersection of the circles.  From (1) with \(M=(m,0), N=(n,0)\) we can solve for \(x_A\).  Subtracting the equations gives  

\[
(n-m)x_A = \frac{b^2-a^2}{2} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad
x_A = \frac{b^2-a^2}{2d}. \tag{16}
\]

But note that this expression uses the original coordinate system where the midpoint of \(AB\) is the origin, not after translation.  After translation, the coordinates change.  However, we can still use these relations because the translation is just a shift, and the vectors from \(I\) to the points are preserved relative to \(I\).  The vector \(\mathbf{a}\) is the position vector of \(A\) after translation.  We can compute its components in the original coordinate system and then translate.  But we need to find \(\mu\) in terms of \(a,b\) without reference to the original midpoint.  Let's approach differently: Since we already know that \(I\) lies on \(AP\), we can directly compute the parameter \(\mu\) by using the fact that \(P\) is the circumcentre.  We have expressions for \(x_P\) and \(x_A\).  So \(\mu = \frac{x_P}{x_A}\).  That's a scalar.  So we can compute \(\mu\) using the formulas (3) and (7) in the original coordinate system (before translation).  However, after translation, the coordinates are shifted, but the relation \(\mu = \frac{x_P}{x_A}\) still holds if we use the coordinates of \(A\) and \(P\) in the original system, because the translation does not affect the collinearity with origin? Wait, after translation, \(I\) becomes origin.  In that system, the coordinates of \(A\) and \(P\) are \(\mathbf{a}\) and \(\mu\mathbf{a}\).  The scalar \(\mu\) is the ratio of the vectors.  That is the same as the ratio of the signed distances along the line \(AP\).  So we can compute \(\mu\) from the original coordinates by evaluating \(\frac{x_P}{x_A}\) (or the ratio of the vectors).  But we need to be careful: In the original system (with midpoint of AB as origin), \(A\) and \(P\) are not necessarily collinear with \(I\)? We proved they are collinear with \(I\).  So indeed, \(A\), \(I\), \(P\) are collinear.  That means that the vectors \(A-I\) and \(P-I\) are parallel.  The ratio \(\mu\) is the scalar such that \(P-I = \mu (A-I)\).  So \(\mu\) can be expressed as \(\frac{x_P - I_x}{x_A - I_x}\) (provided \(x_A \neq I_x\)).  That is not simply \(\frac{x_P}{x_A}\).  But in our translation to make \(I\) the origin, we subtract \(I\).  So the coordinates become \(A' = A-I\) and \(P' = P-I\).  Then \(P' = \mu A'\).  So \(\mu\) is the ratio of the vectors after subtraction.  So we can compute \(\mu\) from the original coordinates after subtracting \(I\).  That's fine.

  However, maybe we can find \(\mu\) more elegantly: Since \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(ACD\), and we have the expression for \(y_P\) in terms of \(A\) and \(x_P\).  We also have that \(I\) is on \(AP\).  Using the homothety relations (11), we can derive that \(P\) is the image of \(A\) under a certain homothety? Actually we have \(F = -\frac{b}{a}A\).  In the numeric, \(P=F\).  But in general, \(P\) is not equal to \(F\).  So we need to determine \(\mu\).  Let's compute \(\mu\) directly using the formulas we have, but now in the translated system where \(I=0\).  We'll express everything in terms of \(a,b\) and the vector \(\mathbf{a}\).  The point \(C\) and \(D\) are on the \(x\)-axis.  We can also express \(M\) and \(N\) in terms of \(d\) and \(a,b\) as before.  Then we can compute the circumcentre \(P\) of \(ACD\) in that system.  That will involve \(A\) (vector), \(C\), \(D\).  Since \(A\) is some vector on \(\ell\), we need to relate it to \(M\) and \(N\).  But we can also compute \(P\) directly using coordinates and then find \(\mu\) such that \(P = \mu A\).  That seems messy but doable.

  However, we can avoid explicit \(\mu\) by using the fact that we already have a relation between the points on \(\ell\) and the homothety.  The key is to prove that the line through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) (which is \(\ell\)) is tangent to the circle \((BEF)\).  Since we have expressions for \(B,E,F\) in terms of \(\mathbf{a}\), and also we can compute \(H\) (orthocentre of \(PMN\)) in terms of \(M,N,P\) and then express \(L\) (line through \(H\) parallel to \(\ell\)) as a line with direction \(\mathbf{a}\).  The condition for tangency can be expressed as the distance from the circumcenter \(O\) of \((BEF)\) to \(L\) equals the radius.  Perhaps we can find a simple expression for \(O\) in this translated system.

  Let's compute the circle \((BEF)\).  Points:

  \[
  A = \mathbf{a},\quad E = -\frac{a}{b}\mathbf{a},\quad F = -\frac{b}{a}\mathbf{a},\quad B = \overline{\mathbf{a}}.
  \]

  Since \(\mathbf{a}\) is not necessarily horizontal, we denote \(\mathbf{a}=(x,y)\) with \(y>0\).  Then \(B=(x,-y)\).  \(E\) and \(F\) are scalar multiples of \(\mathbf{a}\).  

  The line \(\ell\) (through origin) is the set \(\{t\mathbf{a}\}\).  So \(E\) and \(F\) lie on \(\ell\).  

  We want to show that the line \(L\) through \(H\) parallel to \(\ell\) is tangent to the circumcircle of \(BEF\).  This is reminiscent of the fact that if a triangle has two vertices on a line through a point and the third vertex is the reflection across a line, then the circle passing through those three points is tangent to a line through the orthocentre parallel to that line.  Perhaps we can find the circumcenter \(O\) of \(BEF\) explicitly.

  Since \(E\) and \(F\) are collinear with the origin, the perpendicular bisector of \(EF\) is a line perpendicular to \(\mathbf{a}\).  Let’s compute the midpoint of \(EF\):

  \[
  M_{EF} = \frac{E+F}{2} = -\frac{ \frac{a}{b} + \frac{b}{a} }{2}\,\mathbf{a} = -\frac{a^2+b^2}{2ab}\,\mathbf{a}.
  \]

  The perpendicular bisector is the set of points \(X\) such that \((X-M_{EF})\cdot \mathbf{a}=0\).  

  Now consider the circumcenter \(O\).  It must satisfy  

  \[
  |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2 \quad\text{and}\quad |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2.
  \]

  The first equality gives \((O-M_{EF})\cdot \mathbf{a}=0\) (since \(EF\) is parallel to \(\mathbf{a}\)).  So \(O\) lies on the line perpendicular to \(\mathbf{a}\) passing through \(M_{EF}\).  This line is not yet determined.  The second equality gives a relation involving \(B\).  

  Perhaps we can find \(O\) as the image of the orthocentre of something under a translation.  Let's attempt to solve for \(O\).  

  Let’s write \(O = \alpha \mathbf{a} + \beta \mathbf{a}^\perp\), where \(\mathbf{a}^\perp\) is a vector perpendicular to \(\mathbf{a}\) of the same length.  Since \(\mathbf{a}\) is not necessarily horizontal, we can use a coordinate system with basis \(\mathbf{a}\) and \(\mathbf{a}^\perp\).  Then we can express all points in that basis.

  Let \(\mathbf{u} = \frac{\mathbf{a}}{|\mathbf{a}|}\).  Then \(\mathbf{a} = |\mathbf{a}| \mathbf{u}\).  Define \(\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{u}^\perp\) (rotate by 90°).  Then we have coordinates:  

  \[
  \mathbf{a} = (|\mathbf{a}|, 0) \text{ in } (\mathbf{u},\mathbf{v}) \text{ coordinates}.
  \]  

  So we can think of the plane as ℝ² with axes along \(\mathbf{u}\) and \(\mathbf{v}\).  Then points are represented by coordinates (x,y) where x is the coordinate along \(\mathbf{u}\) and y along \(\mathbf{v}\).  In this coordinate system:

  - \(A = (|\mathbf{a}|, 0)\).
  - \(E = -\frac{a}{b} |
